    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1237099099844061343">Ignorance is bliss, or so hopes Antoine, the lead character in Martin Page's stinging satire, "How I Became Stupid" a modern day "Candide" with a Darwin Award-like sensibility. A twenty-five-year-old Aramaic scholar, Antoine has had it with being brilliant and deeply self-aware in today's culture. So tortured is he by the depth of his perception and understanding of himsel</span>
  <span id="freeText1237099099844061343" style="display:none">Ignorance is bliss, or so hopes Antoine, the lead character in Martin Page's stinging satire, "How I Became Stupid" a modern day "Candide" with a Darwin Award-like sensibility. A twenty-five-year-old Aramaic scholar, Antoine has had it with being brilliant and deeply self-aware in today's culture. So tortured is he by the depth of his perception and understanding of himself and the world around him that he vows to denounce his intelligence by any means necessary in order to become stupid enough to be a happy, functioning member of society. What follows is a dark and hilarious odyssey as Antoine tries everything from alcoholism to stock-trading in order to lighten the burden of his brain on his soul.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1237099099844061343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41979597?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2009</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41979597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8985513583518033992">EN<br/>although based on an interesting idea and quite well reasoned, the book seems overrated; except for antoine all the other characters lack personality. the last chapter is almost annoying, due to the artificial feeling and the beginning of the love story. the naivety and the simplicity of the 2 characters is so exaggerated so that everything becomes unnatural. <br/><br/>RO<br/>desi bazata pe o idee interesanta si argumentata destul de bine, cartulia mi se pare cam overrated; cu exceptia lui antoine, personaje</span>

<div id="review_233156145" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/233156145" />
    <a title="Printable Tire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59506-printable-tire"><img alt="Printable Tire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226430554p2/59506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/233156145?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Printable Tire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Printable Tire" href="/user/show/59506-printable-tire">Printable Tire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer233156145" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16277806385557220731">I'm thinking about instituting a new book-reading rule next year: stop reading books as soon as they uninterest me. Were that it was that new year, because this piece of shit would have been a good book to start stopping with.<br/><br/>In actuality, I've been quitting books for years: I just don't write reviews for them because I usually stop reading them by the first or second page, and who am I to judge if a book might not get better as it goes on? But this book, this "book" I knew I was going to hate b</span>
  <span id="freeText16277806385557220731" style="display:none">I'm thinking about instituting a new book-reading rule next year: stop reading books as soon as they uninterest me. Were that it was that new year, because this piece of shit would have been a good book to start stopping with.<br /><br />In actuality, I've been quitting books for years: I just don't write reviews for them because I usually stop reading them by the first or second page, and who am I to judge if a book might not get better as it goes on? But this book, this "book" I knew I was going to hate by page 1. Yet I kept reading. Why? Mainly because it was short enough, mainly because I was intrigued it if could really be that bad. <br /><br />It was bad.<br /><br />I've always thought quitting a book to be a cop-out, an apology to our ADHD-Driven world. But really, what is the point in finishing a book that sucks? Is something gained from the experience, or would our time be better spent elsewhere? This is what I set out to find out.<br /><br />Like I said, I knew exactly where this book was going right away and I didn't like it. Here would be a joyless, smug ride though flippant jokes and superficial, staged scenarios. <br /><br />Ever since reading a certain review in the New Yorker many Sundays back, I have been experiencing "funny" books in a new, party-pooper fashion. You can only see the begining of the review here <br />(<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.newyorker.com/arts/critics/books/2010/11/08/101108crbo_books_wood">http://www.newyorker.com/arts/critics...</a>) but if the chance arrives I would search it out. Basically, Wood's problem with "funny" books is that they lack "believability:" a man chases an ostrich through his living room and Woods thinks, "No, he didn't." So it was with me in reading this book: narrator does something obnoxious (has dinner routinely with his quirky, barely sketched-out friends at an Icelandic restaurant, for example) and all I can think (besides "Why am I reading this?") is, "No, he didn't."<br /><br />I guess I shouldn't complain. The second-rate "crazy-random" humor on display in How I became Stupid is a formula my own writing often falls into, but the fact of the matter is as used here, it simply isn't funny. It's somehow predictably random, and nothing can be worse than a predictable joke. <br /><br />(I should mention one "funny" scenario is at least plausible, that being the protagonist visiting his pediatrician at a relatively "elderly" age, that being 25. I too went to my pediatrician until my parent's health insurance finally ran out, somewhere along the age of 24. Let's just say my genital warts were far from his usual area of expertise! ((Or so I hope)) (((Okay I'm kidding about that last part))) ((((or am I?)))) ((((no, no, I am))))).<br /><br />If a book isn't believable, it should at least be funny. If a book isn't funny, it should at least by insightful. If a book isn't insightful, it should at least be stylistically competent. This book is irredeemable: it offers nothing, save for a few nice descriptions anyone could come up with if they sat down long enough and the insights and moral-posturings of an unremarkable, talentless 24-year year-old Frenchman.<br /><br />Despite the familiar, unrealistic jokes, the rotating cast of uninspiring stereotypes, pretentious conversations, dull witticisms, and corny attempts at feeling, every possibly Big Issue of Modern-Day Life is smugged over in the most superficial, snarky, high-horse way possible. I've read Pet Columnists in Free Magazines with more interesting and original (and thankfully more humble) things to say about Modern Life.<br /><br />I'm not going to get into how much of a colossally boring, whiny, snobby twerp the "protagonist" (author) is, but I think this one quote, taken practically at random, shall suffice:<br /><br />"'But we could substitute another game that my neighbors introduced me to. It's called Monopoly. The aim of the game is very simple: you have to get money, be crafty with it... behave like the perfect, idiotic capitalist. It's facinating. One of the virtues of the game is that, in its own playful way, it should teach me- and perhaps even convert me to- a liberal-minded morality. I will adhere to something that, at the moment, I condemn as a simple game, and I won't worry about the crippling rents that put so many families on the streets. I'll become selfish and penny-pinching, and the only thing I'll worry about will be money, the only thing that will matter to me, my only big existential question, will be how I can get as much of it as possible.'"<br /><br />Don't you just want to strangle this fucker by the neck? I'm not sure I could be in the same room with this guy without at least slapping him in the face, and here I read a whole novel of his stupid, smarmy thoughts.<br /><br />In the begining of the book, the protagonist attempts various "hilarious" methods at being happy: alcholosim, suicide. Even as horrible as this book is, this section STILL should have been cut from the text, but I kept hoping one of the methods the protagonist would come upon would be to get the shit kicked mercilessly out of him, so at least I could incur some depleting, meta-happiness from the act.<br /><br />I'm not even going to get into how badly written the book is, how heavy-handed and self-righteous the trackless prose is, or how one quirky, forgettable character cutely speaks in verse, but we are never shown him speaking in verse: we are TOLD he speaks in verse, as if that's enough. Also, he's radioactive and glows in the dark. Isn't that just fucking adorable?<br /><br />(Here's where I'd talk about how the French are never really funny because they try too hard but I'll save my humor-jingoism for another time.)<br /><br />Ultimately, I didn't get anything out of this book: it actually got worse as it went along (and it never really got along), and it WAS a complete waste of my time: the writing wasn't funny, the insights were uninsightful, the action boring yet unbelievable, the narrator nauseating, the ending abysmal. As a matter of fact, I'm tempted to skip over to the Continent to deliver to the author a well-deserved kick in his smug little balls. Yes, I was THAT personally insulted by this crap.<br /><br />But hey, maybe I did get a little something out of all this: I wrote a scathing review, though many years too late, many years too long after this shitty little missive was farted out to a gullible European audience, many years too long after we in the US got a sloppy-second whiff of this precious little wretched turd.<br /><br />It's too easy, but I gotta say it: HOW I became STUPID may not have made me stupid, but it certainly tried its best.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16277806385557220731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

           <span class="uitext reviewText">Any non-cynic with an hour to kill.</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11452263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11807715901256459457">Are you morose? Does your intelligence get in the way of your happiness? Then move along, this book is not for you.<br/><br/>Draw an equilateral triangle. Label each vertex: "Candide", by Voltaire; "<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11.The_Hitchhiker_s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy__Hitchhiker_s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy_Trilogy__Book_1_' title='The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy (Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy Trilogy, Book 1) by Douglas Adams' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy</a>", by Douglas Adams; "Venus on the Half-Shell", by Kilgore Trout.<br/><br/>Now plot a point in the middle of the triangle, but a little off-center, somewhere between Hitchhiker's Guide and Half-Shell. That is where this novel wants to be, in pattern and poise, but is not.<br/><br/>If you want a</span>
  <span id="freeText11807715901256459457" style="display:none">Are you morose? Does your intelligence get in the way of your happiness? Then move along, this book is not for you.<br /><br />Draw an equilateral triangle. Label each vertex: "Candide", by Voltaire; "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11.The_Hitchhiker_s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy__Hitchhiker_s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy_Trilogy__Book_1_" title="The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy (Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy Trilogy, Book 1) by Douglas Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy</a>", by Douglas Adams; "Venus on the Half-Shell", by Kilgore Trout.<br /><br />Now plot a point in the middle of the triangle, but a little off-center, somewhere between Hitchhiker's Guide and Half-Shell. That is where this novel wants to be, in pattern and poise, but is not.<br /><br />If you want an introspective adventure juxtaposing intelligence and emotional awareness, that leaves you with something to discuss, read some Herman Hesse.<br /><br />--spoiler--<br /><br />Despite his journey through the land of unselfconsciousness, Antoine remains unfulfilled. In the end, the answer to his problems is a woman he meets in a park. In fact, she is the one who approaches him, having noticed some quality in his character.<br /><br />So, then, what is this book telling us? Not to pursue, but to wait for the Universe to send us a gift of companionship? I reject it.<br /><br />--end spoiler--<br /><br />I would give this book one star, but it helped me recall some pleasant memories of Europe. For that, and its having a tone utterly and refreshingly lacking in guile, I give it another star.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1625965850" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2161939541391475975">This one was an interesting easy read.<br/><br/>I don't usually read books like this, but I had it in my personal library so I gave it a try. At first I liked it, even though I became aware of this book's main idea when I was like 12 years old, so it wasn't anything new. I still think sometimes if I'd prefer being stupid but happy. <br/><br/>The main theme of this book is the relationship between intelligence and happiness. <b>Antoine</b>, the main character, is not a realistic person, but an idealistic one (i.e. he can't</span>
  <span id="freeText2161939541391475975" style="display:none">This one was an interesting easy read.<br /><br />I don't usually read books like this, but I had it in my personal library so I gave it a try. At first I liked it, even though I became aware of this book's main idea when I was like 12 years old, so it wasn't anything new. I still think sometimes if I'd prefer being stupid but happy. <br /><br />The main theme of this book is the relationship between intelligence and happiness. <b>Antoine</b>, the main character, is not a realistic person, but an idealistic one (i.e. he can't enjoy the sunny weather because he thinks about how the Sun harms african people's crops &amp; is concerned about every possible world problem). One day he decides the source of his sadness is intelligence, and tries to solve that problem, eventually deciding he wants to become stupid. <br /><br />I liked the fact that this book features some interesting philosophies, such as <b>Gustave Flaubert</b>'s metaphor of an intellectual wanting to count the grains of sand on a beach, when he should just walk along the water.<br /><br />I didn't like that this book could've been way shorter than 160 pages (yes I know it's already short, but there are many useless paragraphs in the book).<br /><br />Overall, it's a nice book, but nothing extraordinary. Just a nice synthesis of some interesting but pretty basic ideas the society is confronting with.<br /> <br /> </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_349395" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/349395" />
    <a title="Jared Busch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32989-jared-busch"><img alt="Jared Busch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175129482p2/32989.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/349395?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jared Busch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jared Busch" href="/user/show/32989-jared-busch">Jared Busch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">eh</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32989-jared-busch?shelf=ihatethesebooks">ihatethesebooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer349395" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15744725572093808534">one of the best concepts ever... intelligent guy decides the reason he's miserable is because he's TOO intelligent, so decides to become stupid. HOWEVER, much like the shitty films of M. Night Shyamalan, it fails to live up to the brilliance of its premise. I wish I could tell you specific reasons why I ended up flinging it at the wall, but I've shut it out to make way for more useful things in my brain.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

Hilarious. Yes, the humor is dark.<br /><br />The end is bizarre.<br /><br />(I've just read the other reviews. There is so much hate for this book!         <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1342117895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5773301672118689389">I am hugely attracted to books set in France, especially when they are written by French authors. This is what happens when you are a Francophile. Unfortunately, being a Francophile also leads me down garden paths and allows me to dip myself into some seriously underwhelming novels. This is one of these novels.<br/><br/>The idea is interesting, but that is where it stops. The characters start off interesting - such as Antoine's friend who can speak only in rhyme - but they are only ever introduced; no one</span>
  <span id="freeText5773301672118689389" style="display:none">I am hugely attracted to books set in France, especially when they are written by French authors. This is what happens when you are a Francophile. Unfortunately, being a Francophile also leads me down garden paths and allows me to dip myself into some seriously underwhelming novels. This is one of these novels.<br /><br />The idea is interesting, but that is where it stops. The characters start off interesting - such as Antoine's friend who can speak only in rhyme - but they are only ever introduced; no one is developed or given any personality at all. This includes Antoine, our protagonist. <br /><br />The last chapter is simply ridiculous, giving us the beginning of a rather indie love story and then ending abruptly. <br /><br />Please don't go near this.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3151380176" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3151380176" />
    <a title="Daniel Allen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/93246388-daniel-allen"><img alt="Daniel Allen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585385925p2/93246388.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3151380176?book_show_action=true">Feb 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Allen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Allen" href="/user/show/93246388-daniel-allen">Daniel Allen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3151380176" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10155498280480982071">Yeah, um, what the fuck? I started and promptly finished this book in one evening, it took me something like two hours. Obviously, it must have some redeeming qualities if I finished it in one sitting — what those are, though, is still a mystery to me. <br/>	I love and hate this book simultaneously. Though, hate is too strong a word to use, and automatically garners some form of artistic gravitas; so let me say, I didn’t care for it at times. The plot follows a young man named Antoine who, being the </span>
  <span id="freeText10155498280480982071" style="display:none">Yeah, um, what the fuck? I started and promptly finished this book in one evening, it took me something like two hours. Obviously, it must have some redeeming qualities if I finished it in one sitting — what those are, though, is still a mystery to me. <br />	I love and hate this book simultaneously. Though, hate is too strong a word to use, and automatically garners some form of artistic gravitas; so let me say, I didn’t care for it at times. The plot follows a young man named Antoine who, being the big smart boy that he is, decides that life is meaningless and that the cause for his unending neurosis might be his anxious predisposition. His friends — also fellow intellectual oddballs (some with some, really, really fucking quirky traits, like glowing in the dark) — agree with him. Antoine’s solution — being the massive intellectual that he is — is to first commit to alcoholism, then suicide, and finally, trying to become stupid. One of his friends, a doctor, gives him some pills that help him worry less: essentially off-brand Oxycontin. Antoine then becomes a high-flying, Wolf of Wall Street type douchebag and then gets visited by a ghost which tells him that he has lost himself. And then, to tie a ribbon on this dog-shit of a plot, we have 5 pages of sudden romance. Holy fuck. <br />	This book reads like it was written by Vonnegut and the group of people who genuinely enjoy the Big Bang Theory and believe that the show’s representation of smart people is true to life. The twist is, every time Vonnegut tries to write out a sentence, he gets decked with a brick. He’s also drunk. And on coke. Meanwhile, the co-author is just being paid by the word, and so writers these long rambling clauses (kind of this like one right now, see how I’m adding words without adding any content whatsoever? It’s truly a form of art, isn’t it?). There are some truly nice sentences there, and some of the ideas are good too. I found myself nodding along to some of the monologues, the ad nauseam spinning hamster wheel in the brain, for example. But other times — most of the time — it was just plain fucking insufferable. <br />	My biggest problem with the book is two things: plot, and writing. In terms of plot, it is inconsistent. We learn about the protagonist’s heritage, but not a single time does this ever contribute in any way shape or form to the plot. We also get one of Antoine’s best friends, a doctor, who provides him with the medications, but only if he comes to monthly checkups to make sure nothing goes wrong. As we learn, everything goes quite terribly wrong, and yet, Antoine continues to take his red pills because I guess the doctor just gave up. Also, Antoine is wholly dependent on some neighbor’s daughter to go to the doctor, but we never hear more about that ever again. So fuck consistency. I guess. Furthermore, and this is what I’m saying with poor Vonnegut getting continuously decked as he types out his sentences, things happen for no apparent reason. And don’t try and tell me it’s ‘all about the absurdist possibilities in life’ because I know damn well how great those stylistic devices work in books, but in this case, IT ADDS NOTHING. What does it matter that I-Forget-Who got crushed by a group of German tourists? This is reminiscent of many great absurdist science fiction writers, but it was just done…poorly. <br />	Furthermore, the pacing was downright atrocious. Antoine’s whole arc of becoming a Wall Street Douchebag happens through 3 chapters. And then, guess what, his arc back down, his fall from grace? Literally two pages. And it gets better. His arc was completed by a ghost of something or other coming into his house, eating his food, and telling him to get his shit straight. Then he gets kidnapped by his old friends, all wearing quirky Albert Einstein masks — as one does. Last but not least, adding nothing to the plot, and only stroking the public’s need for romance, we get the most compressed, cliched, unbelievable and superficial love interest in the history of books I have read. By the end of the book, everyone lives happily ever after. No matter the financial crisis Antoine caused (even though we take great pain to learn of how moral he is), or the fact that his friend got arrested, or that he is still broke with no job and no money (anymore), or the fact that his friend the doctor disappeared off the face of the earth. <br />	Whatever, the writer got to pat himself on the back every couple of sentences for being oh so witty and clever. Isn’t it really just much nicer to read something really simple, and notice that the idea behind it goes much deeper than one initially imagined, than to slog through sentences which pause every couple of words to jerk itself off on being ‘high art’, and then realizing there are no actual ideas behind all the veiled pretentiousness and pomposity? I think so. <br />	In the words of Vonnegut: <br />	Poo-twee-tweet? Chirped the bird. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10155498280480982071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1653591020" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1653591020" />
    <a title="Briane Pagel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15929427-briane-pagel"><img alt="Briane Pagel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432603483p2/15929427.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1653591020?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Briane Pagel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Briane Pagel" href="/user/show/15929427-briane-pagel">Briane Pagel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1653591020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9425814443923112044">How I Became Stupid was the oldest book on my to-be-read list; I added it to my Amazon wishlist in June 2010, so it's been up there for about 6 years.<br/><br/>One of the reasons I stopped buying books in bulk, getting 2 or 3 or 4 at a time (aside from the sheer cost) was that by the time I get around to reading a book, I might not be in the mood to read it anymore. Some books have to hit me at the right time; I might think today that a book is going to be awesome but 6 months later my tastes have changed</span>
  <span id="freeText9425814443923112044" style="display:none">How I Became Stupid was the oldest book on my to-be-read list; I added it to my Amazon wishlist in June 2010, so it's been up there for about 6 years.<br /><br />One of the reasons I stopped buying books in bulk, getting 2 or 3 or 4 at a time (aside from the sheer cost) was that by the time I get around to reading a book, I might not be in the mood to read it anymore. Some books have to hit me at the right time; I might think today that a book is going to be awesome but 6 months later my tastes have changed.<br /><br />It's not just the passage of time that makes a book lose its luster. Sometimes a book that sits on a shelf for a while next to my bed will just start to seem like old news and I won't want to read it anymore. It's like they get stale. I can't really explain it.<br /><br />So while I was reading How I Became Stupid, and to be honest not totally enjoying it, I was trying to think of what might have made me want to read it in the first place. At this point, 6 years on, I can't recall how I even first heard of the book.<br /><br />How I Became Stupid is billed as an "International Cult Favorite," and promised hilarity and "coinage and invention." It's translated from French; the author is not the Martin Page who cowrote We Built This City, but some French guy.<br /><br />The basic plot is a guy named Antoine decides that he's too smart and that his intelligence is getting in the way of his enjoying life. So he decides to make himself dumb. First he tries to become an alcoholic, then he decides to kill himself but chickens out after taking a class in suicide. Then he talks to a doctor about getting a lobotomy before getting a prescription for antidepressants. Taking a double dose of these leads him to like McDonalds and get a stock broker job. Once at that job, he twice spills coffee on his computer, and each time the short circuit causes a stock trade that makes him millions. He coasts in this job for a month or so before his old friend (who set him up with the job) tries to get him to go on a double-date with some escorts. At that point, Antoine's 'real' friends kidnap him and deprogram him with voodoo and Flaubert letters. Plus there's a visit from a ghost and, in the end, he meets a girl who makes him stand in the street until a car almost hits them, then pushes him aside and said she saves his life.<br /><br />Aside from the intellectual snobbery of saying that people can be either smart or happy in modern life, and that dulling one's intellect is the only way to be happy, the book is mostly dull rather than offensive -- or 'inventive.' It feels almost like a mad lib plot, and while it touches on themes that are meant to feel significant, it rarely deals with them in either a thoughtful or entertaining way.<br /><br />The best character in the book is a woman in a body cast. Antoine meets her when he tries his first beer and drops into an alcoholic coma after one sip; she is in the hospital because she keeps trying to commit suicide but fails. In her latest, she jumped off the Eiffel Tower and fell on some German tourists. I'd rather have followed her story than Antoine's. <br /><br />The book tries to be madcap but mostly fails. One friend glows in the dark and can only speak in poetry, a quirk that might work better if the author had bothered to write a poem when the man spoke instead of just saying he was speaking in poetry. Antoine's aunt and uncle visit him in the hospital; they are patients there, too, and are convinced that the doctor has taken their spleens and replaced them with inferior spleens.<br /><br />Antoine's awakening, or whatever, is accomplished without Antoine learning anything at all. I guess that was the point of the book, as Antoine announces in the beginning that his experiment in becoming stupid isn't intended to make him a better person.<br /><br />But if you're going to write a book that wants to seem like it is asking a serious intellectual question about the role of intelligence vs. happiness, perhaps you ought to address the question? And if you're going to write a screwball intellectual comedy, like Jerry Lewis seen through Wes Anderson's eyes, you should make it funny and weird, instead of just a random assortment of things that seem designed to seem weird without the weirdness mattering.<br /><br />It's a Potemkin village of a book, just a front of intellectualism and comedy with nothing real behind it. Ironically, it's exactly the kind of book that would be a cult hit in the world Page presents in the book: a world where people aren't smart enough to realize that things aren't as great as they think the are. <br /><br />But in the real world, this book isn't worth waiting six minutes for, let alone six years</span>
  <a data-text-id="9425814443923112044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1550407564" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1550407564" />
    <a title="E. C. Koch" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24562513-e-c-koch"><img alt="E. C. Koch" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542319414p2/24562513.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1550407564?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="E. C. Koch" class="user" itemprop="url" name="E. C. Koch" href="/user/show/24562513-e-c-koch">E. C. Koch</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to E. C. by: </span>
      RJ
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1550407564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12333059321639879370">This is a weird little book that can best be described as, well, French. Each major scene, roughly circumscribed within an unnumbered, untitled chapter, proceeds with little to zero preamble or exposition, which means that you just kind of have to accept that what occurs has a logic beyond that of narrative (because man you could pretty much rearrange what happens here and get the same effect). So then what are we left with? We're left with a sort of long meditation on what it would be like to w</span>
  <span id="freeText12333059321639879370" style="display:none">This is a weird little book that can best be described as, well, French. Each major scene, roughly circumscribed within an unnumbered, untitled chapter, proceeds with little to zero preamble or exposition, which means that you just kind of have to accept that what occurs has a logic beyond that of narrative (because man you could pretty much rearrange what happens here and get the same effect). So then what are we left with? We're left with a sort of long meditation on what it would be like to walk back into Plato's cave, seeking bliss in ignorance instead of being depressed in knowledge. And in that way this feels like a novel(la) of the moment, which is a statement that wants a little unpacking. Ours is a time in which data is all around us all the time and the internet has shaped (and continues to shape) our sociocultural landscape and we're overexposed to visual/aural media and what this all amounts to is that we have to cast off way more discreet bits of information than any one of us could ever actually process. Living out of the cave means having to endure this constant bombardment and then also to choose which data will permeate our intellectual membranes and become "what we know." This is such a difficult - even Sisyphean - task that it's a lot easier to turn off our minds and turn on the TV or get drunk or become savagely polar in our political understanding of the world; in a word, to become imbalanced. What we call intelligence in the 21st century (and I think this is what Page is getting at here) is the ability to maintain balance, both in one's personality and the data that are allowed to pass one's membrane. The paradox built into this so-called balance, this ability to see equally each side of a question or argument, is that an intelligent person must simultaneously be totally imbalanced, as in all brain and all objectivity all of the time. This imbalanced balance, as portrayed in this book, is crushing and it's depressing, and when your antennae are always tuned to empathy you're going to experience your intelligence as pain and if this lasts too long then measures like suicide and alcoholism and medication start to look like really nice alternatives. This book's main character, Antoine, ultimately tries to mitigate his pain by becoming like "everyone else," which here means a capitalist consumer popping Happyzac about a dozen times a day who goes to work as a broker after leaving his lecturing position at a university. And he comes to feel neither good nor bad about anything. He replaces thinking with making money and just sort of lives without emotion, and then it's pretty much left up to the reader to decide whether he made the right choices or not. There is an attempt at redemption at the end that I basically found to be unconvincing given what preceded it, but maybe I'm just cynical. This is a quick read that will give back to you as much as you put into it, and will, I think, leave you wishing Page had put in a little more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12333059321639879370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2678880775" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<div id="review_669015649" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/669015649" />
    <a title="Melody Kimberly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15334034-melody-kimberly"><img alt="Melody Kimberly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540702112p2/15334034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/669015649?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melody Kimberly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melody Kimberly" href="/user/show/15334034-melody-kimberly">Melody Kimberly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer669015649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8327391676970345775">One thing: Don't get fooled by the book title... this book is everything other than stupid. =) I took an overdose of delight reading it in one sitting at night. There's so much to learn in a whole new perspective about intelligence and stupidity, you'll be able to draw a definite line between these two aspects but both of which have their own pros and cons. It is also studded with morals confronting the society today especially within the sphere of self-discovery, taking risk and choices, fittin</span>
  <span id="freeText8327391676970345775" style="display:none">One thing: Don't get fooled by the book title... this book is everything other than stupid. =) I took an overdose of delight reading it in one sitting at night. There's so much to learn in a whole new perspective about intelligence and stupidity, you'll be able to draw a definite line between these two aspects but both of which have their own pros and cons. It is also studded with morals confronting the society today especially within the sphere of self-discovery, taking risk and choices, fitting in, money, friendships and so much more (along with professional undertakings in the business imperium). You can draw a lot of learning from it (especially to those who think they're so smart, they're at the top of the world, here's a book that thinks he's cursed for being one, it might shed a different light on you)<br /><br /><br />And also, I would like to share one significant learning I exhausted from this book: <br />The solution to some life problems is not always the reverse of it, such as turning the burden of intelligence to the bliss of ignorance. Because life is more about striking the balance, not too much on the other side...not to be too pessimistic as well too optimistic, too all-knowing nor too unknowing, too elated nor too depressed, to go over thinking nor go shallow on things...find your center, let go of your worries that instead of counting the grains of sand, just 'take a walk on the beach'. <br />Anyway, there's so much about this book. I highly recommend it for anyone looking for a fresh and inventive novel. =)</span>
  <a data-text-id="8327391676970345775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Michel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michel" href="/user/show/1429477-michel">Michel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34579410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12947067177517643712">A thoughtful, sarcastic, sad, hilarious, disturbing, lovely little book.<br/>Antoine decides to stop thinking and heed Flaubert's injunction: To be stupid, selfish and healthy are the three requirements to be happy, though if stupidity is lacking, all is lost.<br/>To be happy, he will strip himself of his social conscience, his thoughtful sensitivity, his few friends, even "his precious and individual limited editions", books painfully reconstituted, page by page.<br/>Further to buck his crippling awareness an</span>
  <span id="freeText12947067177517643712" style="display:none">A thoughtful, sarcastic, sad, hilarious, disturbing, lovely little book.<br />Antoine decides to stop thinking and heed Flaubert's injunction: To be stupid, selfish and healthy are the three requirements to be happy, though if stupidity is lacking, all is lost.<br />To be happy, he will strip himself of his social conscience, his thoughtful sensitivity, his few friends, even "his precious and individual limited editions", books painfully reconstituted, page by page.<br />Further to buck his crippling awareness and debilitating empathy, he equips himself with all the necessary implements: Happyzak, fastfood, mindless drivel, television shows, gym, a lucky stock trading career, computer dating, and all the gadgets (which he accumulates without using, he does the buying like a pro, just never gets around to the consuming).<br />But he lacks true selfcentered grit: "At one point, he was afraid he might lose his little shy streak and join the great mass of people who despise you if you don't dominate them; but… he managed to save it like an oasis of his personality" and it yanks him right back, to the compassionate misfit he always was, lacking the prejudices necessary to 'belong'. He meets a ghost, then plays one. The end.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12947067177517643712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Una Theia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Una Theia" href="/user/show/4904601-una-theia">Una Theia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4904601-una-theia?shelf=g-prose">g-prose</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer451337964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1608367586102865475">A hideous reading! <br/><br/>We have a main character utterly anguished by his so called intelligence - never and nowhere properly described. And some fucked up issue ('Oh! My intelligence hurts so much!')to be solved with incredibly stupid solutions. <br/><br/>The choices he makes to become stupid are so idiotic and trivial, it's impossible to believe that a decent (not necessarily very intelligent) human being might see some logic in them. <br/><br/>Any hope for this writing to be somewhat cleaner is knocked out by cliches</span>
  <span id="freeText1608367586102865475" style="display:none">A hideous reading! <br /><br />We have a main character utterly anguished by his so called intelligence - never and nowhere properly described. And some fucked up issue ('Oh! My intelligence hurts so much!')to be solved with incredibly stupid solutions. <br /><br />The choices he makes to become stupid are so idiotic and trivial, it's impossible to believe that a decent (not necessarily very intelligent) human being might see some logic in them. <br /><br />Any hope for this writing to be somewhat cleaner is knocked out by cliches and some very boring or horrid contemporary 'belle-lettre' (some frankenstein-metaphors), which make the whole work so unbelievably unoriginal. <br /><br />The definition of intelligence itself is totally fucked up. It means knowledge, morality, political views, some weird socio-cultural choices, when, in fact, intelligence means TO UNDERSTAND, the capacity to understand. <br /><br />On the top of it all stands the Capra-style happy ending. Plus another happy ending (the happy ending is double or triple). <br />The author is totally unable to stop surprising the reader with his vulgar appropriation of intelligence, with his annoying pretense. <br /><br />(And there's more... There's also an anti-ode to McDonald's. Fabulous!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1608367586102865475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484966112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1786265538352829866">A brief, pithy novel about a young French intellectual with a dead-end degree and a dead-end job who decides he'd be happier if he was less intelligent, and sets about trying to make it happen. Eventually it descends into broad antimaterialist satire, and I think it could have benefited from a savvy editor, but nonetheless, I recommend it unreservedly to anyone reading this.<br/><br/>Money quote: "Alcoholics are pitied, they are cared for, they are thought of in medical terms, humanely. But no one thinks </span>
  <span id="freeText1786265538352829866" style="display:none">A brief, pithy novel about a young French intellectual with a dead-end degree and a dead-end job who decides he'd be happier if he was less intelligent, and sets about trying to make it happen. Eventually it descends into broad antimaterialist satire, and I think it could have benefited from a savvy editor, but nonetheless, I recommend it unreservedly to anyone reading this.<br /><br />Money quote: "Alcoholics are pitied, they are cared for, they are thought of in medical terms, humanely. But no one thinks of pitying intelligent people: 'He watches human behavior, that must make him very unhappy'; 'My niece is very intelligent, but she's a really nice girl. She's hoping to grow out of it'; 'For a while there, I was afraid you might become intelligent.' ... [I]ntelligence is a double curse: it makes you suffer, and no one thinks of it as an illness. Being an alcoholic would be a social promotion by comparison."</span>
  <a data-text-id="1786265538352829866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17837234731431530950">As someone who can sometimes feel a higher level of intelligence in comparison to the huddled masses, the idea that someone can somehow become “stupid” in order to make everyday life easier made me chuckle because I’ve felt that way before. I think my favorite part of the book is when the main character decides to try alcoholism, but first he must interview an alcoholic. After all his vain attempts to numb his intelligence, he comes to realize that being smart isn’t so bad after all. It’s a deli</span>
  <span id="freeText17837234731431530950" style="display:none">As someone who can sometimes feel a higher level of intelligence in comparison to the huddled masses, the idea that someone can somehow become “stupid” in order to make everyday life easier made me chuckle because I’ve felt that way before. I think my favorite part of the book is when the main character decides to try alcoholism, but first he must interview an alcoholic. After all his vain attempts to numb his intelligence, he comes to realize that being smart isn’t so bad after all. It’s a delightful read that even the most intelligent person will enjoy.</span>

<div id="review_833446754" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/833446754" />
    <a title="Luke Goldstein" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44432-luke-goldstein"><img alt="Luke Goldstein" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442642700p2/44432.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/833446754?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luke Goldstein" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luke Goldstein" href="/user/show/44432-luke-goldstein">Luke Goldstein</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer833446754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16344465118020710785">With a title like this gracing the front cover, you might get the impression that this is a tale of wrong choices, longing for the good ole’ days and trying to figure out how it all got away.<br/><br/>You’d be mostly wrong…mostly.<br/><br/>How I Became Stupid is a tale about Antoine, who feels forever burdened by his astounding intelligence and natural curiosity about the world he inhabits. The weight of his knowledge is stifling and he longs to become one of the drooling, ignorant masses he sees around him every d</span>
  <span id="freeText16344465118020710785" style="display:none">With a title like this gracing the front cover, you might get the impression that this is a tale of wrong choices, longing for the good ole’ days and trying to figure out how it all got away.<br /><br />You’d be mostly wrong…mostly.<br /><br />How I Became Stupid is a tale about Antoine, who feels forever burdened by his astounding intelligence and natural curiosity about the world he inhabits. The weight of his knowledge is stifling and he longs to become one of the drooling, ignorant masses he sees around him every day. His goal by whatever means necessary is to dumb himself down into apparent nothingness in a crowd. Only then, he thinks, or deduces rather, can he find true happiness. He tries various methods and over-complicated ways to end up in places most people find themselves without thinking at all. This sarcastically comic journey follows these brave attempts to limit the reaches of his mind and the effects it has on those who know him, before and after his inclusion into the world of the stupid. Antoine is a wanderer, a rover, a vagabond of the mind, yearning for a place where his mind doesn’t run free because it sees nothing and nowhere to run to.<br /><br />Martin Page, a French author, created Antoine almost as a reaction and retribution of the world of today. We cling to evolution and parade around preening in front of all other creatures, but not with our feathers or our fur, since we lost those long ago, but we preen with our minds and our reason. As a race we lord our cognitive thought over all other organisms, but Antoine shows us it comes with a hefty price tag. Martin’s novel gives us a glimpse into the mirror, a vision of someone we all hide deep in the closet who judges other people, overthinks each and every detail of the life before his eyes and who has a problem taking anything at face value. The eternal question posed by the book is whether there is a way to tone down that voice in our mind? Reel in the ego and superego and just become one with the mass consciousness, oh, and don’t forget to enjoy it as well.<br /><br />This was a quick and enjoyable read, laced with wry wit, sarcasm and unique characters, people who would have to be incredibly singular just to stand hanging around Antoine in the first place. I felt the lesson I took away was you can never run away from who you really are and to be truly happy you need to start with acceptance of that fact. A tall tale indeed, but one that can be accomplished with a little time, energy and possibly a nice, creamy bar of dark chocolate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16344465118020710785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1285895788" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1285895788" />
    <a title="Nicole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23121713-nicole"><img alt="Nicole" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376567869p2/23121713.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1285895788?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole" href="/user/show/23121713-nicole">Nicole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1285895788" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7303332378584329372">Despite its consistency and it being in the same arena as Andrew Kaufman's work, this was up and down for me in terms of how much I liked it.<br/><br/>There are a fair few quotable bits and the satire is often so on point. However, sometimes it felt sort of unremarkable I guess. But I chalk that up to my reading (and thinking) a lot of similar things already.<br/><br/>The last chapter felt unnecessary. Tonally it fitted but content-wise it seemed an odd, out-of-nowhere <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[romantic <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> additi</span>
  <span id="freeText7303332378584329372" style="display:none">Despite its consistency and it being in the same arena as Andrew Kaufman's work, this was up and down for me in terms of how much I liked it.<br /><br />There are a fair few quotable bits and the satire is often so on point. However, sometimes it felt sort of unremarkable I guess. But I chalk that up to my reading (and thinking) a lot of similar things already.<br /><br />The last chapter felt unnecessary. Tonally it fitted but content-wise it seemed an odd, out-of-nowhere <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[romantic <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> addition. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Especially since Antoine already has people who understood and/or appreciated him as he is - which he is aware of - and there was no indication that the only thing missing in his life is this Manic Pixie Dream Girl to come turn his life around and apparently make him happy/complete/not tormented by his intelligence anymore... It seems to detract a little from an otherwise perfectly fine story and feels a bit like the answer to his self-destructive constant analysis of the world is a romantic distraction...but it's still just a distraction and not any kind of actual answer to his problem: his keen awareness of the world was making him depressed. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> But maybe that's cynical of me.<br /><br />I'd still recommend it though.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25544343" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4552998001876259468">More like a 2.9<br/><br/>While I read the book, my feelings for it were up and down. I read a passage that made we want to give the book rating of 5. Then something else made me want to give the book a 2. later I would read something else that made we want to rate the book a 4. It went on like this until the last word of the book. I wonder how much of the the meaning of the book was lost in translation. Although it should not be much since French is a Romantic language and English is built off the back of</span>
  <span id="freeText4552998001876259468" style="display:none">More like a 2.9<br /><br />While I read the book, my feelings for it were up and down. I read a passage that made we want to give the book rating of 5. Then something else made me want to give the book a 2. later I would read something else that made we want to rate the book a 4. It went on like this until the last word of the book. I wonder how much of the the meaning of the book was lost in translation. Although it should not be much since French is a Romantic language and English is built off the back of Romantic and Germanic languages.<br /><br />The book is a great book for quotes about society. I highlighted a ton of passages. Both the climax and the ending was a little unsettling. It seem to be too easy to solve? I can't think of a good phrase without spoiling the it. Like at the end of a Scooby Doo cartoon when they just start explaining how they knew who the killer was. The very ending is kind of mysterious because i'm not sure what is reality and what is fantasy. <br /><br />Maybe I need to read the book again and not while riding MTA trains.</span>

          </span>

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fFfvzl5hqnJyaZ7+kOYJ9Gi51/4TqOJ8wx58gVLQoFR7GXsRh/JaHDL3/aoQkuvpUZ7/GJxkj05wQB/+/Dq5AQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('59+rG28dcEbxSc5xh94uZTIVAbIYHpo6zrAU/kHyOPrgkT/Eto6AKLHXrSUHqsx4CzIpVJfS9wh97neB7xghrw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/817199.Maitreyi"><img alt="Maitreyi" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1303896468l/817199._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_817199'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/817199.Maitreyi?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Maitreyi<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/14521.Mircea_Eliade?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Mircea Eliade<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.93 avg rating &mdash; 16,704 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1933\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer5467433811761814121\">Set in 1930s Calcutta, this is a roman á clef of remarkable intimacy. Originally published in Romanian in 1933, this semiautobiographical novel by the world renowned scholar Mircea Eliade details the passionate awakenings of Alain, an ambitious young<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText5467433811761814121\" style=\"display:none\">Set in 1930s Calcutta, this is a roman á clef of remarkable intimacy. Originally published in Romanian in 1933, this semiautobiographical novel by the world renowned scholar Mircea Eliade details the passionate awakenings of Alain, an ambitious young French engineer flush with colonial pride and prejudice and full of a European fascination with the mysterious subcontinent. Offered the hospitality of a senior Indian colleague, Alain grasps at the chance to discover the authentic India firsthand. He soon finds himself enchanted by his host\'s daughter, the lovely and inscrutable Maitreyi, a precocious young poet and former student of Tagore. What follows is a charming, tentative flirtation that soon, against all the proprieties and precepts of Indian society, blossoms into a love affair both impossible and ultimately tragic. This erotic passion plays itself out in Alain\'s thoughts long after its bitter conclusion. In hindsight he sets down the story, quoting from the diaries of his disordered days, and trying to make sense of the sad affair. A vibrantly poetic love story, Bengal Nights is also a cruel account of the wreckage left in the wake of a young man\'s self discovery. At once horrifying and deeply moving, Eliade\'s story repeats the patterns of European engagement with India even as it exposes and condemns them. Invaluable for the insight it offers into Eliade\'s life and thought, it is a work of great intellectual and emotional power. &quot;Bengal Nights is forceful and harshly poignant, written with a great love of India informed by clear-eyed understanding. But do not open it if you prefer to remain unmoved by your reading matter.It is enough to make stones weep.&quot; — Literary ReviewMircea Eliade (1907-1986) was the Sewell L. Avery Distinguished Service Professor in the Divinity School and the Committee on Social Thought at the University of Chicago. Many of his scholarly works, as well as his two-volume autobiography and four-volume journal, are published by the University of Chicago Press. Translated into French in 1950, Bengal Nights was an immediate critical success. The film, Les Nuits Bengali, appeared in 1987.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"5467433811761814121\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div În tren sau la operă, în autobuz sau la biserică, în parc sau la teatru, în Parlament sau la bloc, reprezentanţii acestei categorii fondează un cult volatil, fără agendă şi fără orizont. Religia lor e sfidarea celorlalţi. Nesimţitul nu are un cod de reguli, dar se comportă ca şi cum l-ar avea. E invaziv până la ubicuitate, pisălog până la nevroză şi vociferant până la delir. Îl vezi unde nu te aştepţi, îl auzi peste tot, îl adulmeci la fiecare colţ de stradă şi pe fiecare culoar de vagon. Însă nici măcar el nu deţine toate pârghiile meşteşugului. E imperfect şi are nevoie de îndrumări. Exact asta face Ghidul nesimţitului – furnizează reglajele necesare celor ispitiţi de performanţa în domeniu. Sigur, miza lui formativă şi informativă e o glumă. Ghidul nesimţitului vrea în primul rând să amuze. Numai că în spatele tonului ironic al cărţii răsar fapte, întâmplări şi buclucuri de care foarte mulţi dintre noi au avut parte. الجنوبية، قبل أن يعود ويستقرّ في الريف الفرنسي. أبٌ لأربعة أطفال، يكرّس كلماته لخدمة الطبيعة والإنسان، ويناضل من أجل عالم ألطف وأكرم لساكنيه. \nإخفاء<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"213368561133400170\" <span id="freeTextContainer17626619091535353688">French writer Martin Page is the author of the bestselling novel, How I Became Stupid, which won the Euroregional schools’ literature prize, an award given by Belgian, Dutch and German students. His novels have been translated in a dozen languages. He also writes for children (I am an earthquake, Conversation with a chocolate cake…).<br/>He is a recipient of the Akademie Schloss Solitude Fellowship. He</span>
  <span id="freeText17626619091535353688" style="display:none">French writer Martin Page is the author of the bestselling novel, How I Became Stupid, which won the Euroregional schools’ literature prize, an award given by Belgian, Dutch and German students. His novels have been translated in a dozen languages. He also writes for children (I am an earthquake, Conversation with a chocolate cake…).<br />He is a recipient of the Akademie Schloss Solitude Fellowship. He lives in Paris.<br /><br /><br /></span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/180608-comment-je-suis-devenu-stupide">Quotes from How I Became Stupid</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
